ns-0 ns-subject mw-editable page-Early_human_migrations rootpage-Early_human_migrations skin-vector action-view"> <div class="noprint" id="mw-page-base"></div>\n<div class="noprint" id="mw-head-base"></div>\n<div class="mw-body" id="content" role="main">\n<a id="top"></a>\n<div class="mw-body-content" id="siteNotice"><!-- CentralNotice --></div><div class="mw-indicators mw-body-content">\n</div>\n<h1 class="firstHeading" id="firstHeading" lang="en">Early human migrations</h1> <div class="mw-body-content" id="bodyContent">\n<div class="noprint" id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div> <div id="contentSub"></div>\n<div id="jump-to-nav"></div> <a class="mw-jump-link" href="#mw-head">Jump to navigation</a>\n<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#p-search">Jump to search</a>\n<div class="mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr" id="mw-content-text" lang="en"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/History_of_human_migration" title="History of human migration">History of human migration</a> and <a href="/wiki/Pre-modern_human_migration" title="Pre-modern human migration">Pre-modern human migration</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:352px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Putative_migration_waves_out_of_Africa.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1002" data-file-width="1210" decoding="async" height="290" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/73/Putative_migration_waves_out_of_Africa.png/350px-Putative_migration_waves_out_of_Africa.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/73/Putative_migration_waves_out_of_Africa.png/525px-Putative_migration_waves_out_of_Africa.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/73/Putative_migration_waves_out_of_Africa.png/700px-Putative_migration_waves_out_of_Africa.png 2x" width="350"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Putative_migration_waves_out_of_Africa.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Putative migration waves <a href="/wiki/Recent_African_origin_of_modern_humans" title="Recent African origin of modern humans">out of Africa</a> and back migrations into the continent, as well as the locations of major ancient human remains and archeological sites (L&#243;pez et al. 2015).</div></div></div>\n<p><b>Early human migrations</b> are the earliest <a href="/wiki/Human_migration" title="Human migration">migrations and expansions</a> of <a href="/wiki/Homo" title="Homo">archaic and modern humans</a> across continents beginning 2 million years ago with the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Out_of_Africa_I" title="Out of Africa I">out of Africa</a> migration of <i><a href="/wiki/Homo_erectus" title="Homo erectus">Homo erectus</a></i>.  This initial migration was followed by other <a href="/wiki/Archaic_humans" title="Archaic humans">archaic humans</a> including <i><a href="/wiki/Homo_heidelbergensis" title="Homo heidelbergensis">H. heidelbergensis</a></i>, which lived around 500,000 years ago and was the likely ancestor of both <a href="/wiki/Denisovan" title="Denisovan">Denisovans</a> and <a href="/wiki/Neanderthal" title="Neanderthal">Neanderthals</a>.  Early hominids were said to have "crossed land bridges that were eventually covered in water" (History Alive, pub. 2004, TCI).\n</p><p>Within Africa, <i><a href="/wiki/Homo_sapiens" title="Homo sapiens">Homo sapiens</a></i> dispersed around the time of its speciation, roughly 300,000 years ago.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Schlebusch_1-0"><a href="#cite_note-Schlebusch-1">[1]</a></sup>\nThe "<a href="/wiki/Recent_African_origin_of_modern_humans" title="Recent African origin of modern humans">recent African origin</a>" paradigm suggests that the anatomically modern humans outside of Africa descend from a population of <i>Homo sapiens</i> migrating from <a href="/wiki/East_Africa" title="East Africa">East Africa</a> roughly 70,000 years ago and spreading  <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Coastal_migration" title="Coastal migration">along the southern coast</a> of Asia and to Oceania before 50,000 years ago. Modern humans spread <a href="/wiki/European_early_modern_humans" title="European early modern humans">across Europe</a> about 40,000 years ago.\n</p><p>The migrating modern human populations are known to have <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Archaic_human_admixture_with_modern_humans" title="Archaic human admixture with modern humans">interbred</a> with local varieties of archaic humans, so that contemporary human populations are descended in small part (below 10% contribution) from regional varieties of archaic humans.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2"><a href="#cite_note-2">[2]</a></sup>\n</p><p>After the <a href="/wiki/Last_Glacial_Maximum" title="Last Glacial Maximum">Last Glacial Maximum</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ancient_North_Eurasians" title="Ancient North Eurasians">North Eurasian</a> populations migrated <a href="/wiki/Settlement_of_the_Americas" title="Settlement of the Americas">to the Americas</a> about 20,000 years ago.\nNorthern Eurasia was peopled after 12,000 years ago, in the beginning <a href="/wiki/Holocene" title="Holocene">Holocene</a>.\nArctic Canada and Greenland were reached by the <a href="/wiki/Paleo-Eskimo" title="Paleo-Eskimo">Paleo-Eskimo</a> expansion around 4,000 years ago.\nFinally, <a href="/wiki/Polynesia" title="Polynesia">Polynesia</a> was peopled after 2,000 years ago, by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Austronesian_expansion" title="Austronesian expansion">Austronesian expansion</a>.\n</p>\n<div class="toc" id="toc"><input class="toctogglecheckbox" id="toctogglecheckbox" role="button" style="display:none" type="checkbox"/><div class="toctitle" dir="ltr" lang="en"><h2>Contents</h2><span class="toctogglespan"><label class="toctogglelabel" for="toctogglecheckbox"></label></span></div>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-1"><a href="#Early_humans_(before_Homo_sapiens)"><span class="tocnumber">1</span> <span erectus</i></span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-4"><a href="#Homo_sapiens"><span class="tocnumber">2</span> <span class="toctext"><i>Homo sapiens</i></span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-5"><a href="#Dispersal_throughout_Africa"><span class="tocnumber">2.1</span> <span class="toctext">Dispersal throughout Africa</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 href="#Holocene_migrations"><span class="tocnumber">2.6</span> <span class="toctext">Holocene migrations</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-3 tocsection-16"><a href="#Eurasia_2"><span class="tocnumber">2.6.5</span> <span class="toctext">Arctic</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-21"><a href="#See_also"><span class="tocnumber">3</span> <span class="toclevel-1 tocsection-23"><a href="#Further_reading"><span class="tocnumber">5</span> <span class="toctext">Further reading</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-24"><a href="#External_links"><span class="tocnumber">6</span> <span class="toctext">External links</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</div>\n<h2><span id="Early_humans_.28before_Homo_sapiens.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_humans_(before_Homo_sapiens)">Early humans (before <i>Homo sapiens)</i></span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Early_human_migrations&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Early humans (before Homo sapiens)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Early_hominin_expansions_out_of_Africa" title="Early hominin expansions out of Africa">Early hominin expansions out of Africa</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Homo_erectus_new.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="680" data-file-width="544" decoding="async" height="250" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cb/Homo_erectus_new.JPG/200px-Homo_erectus_new.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cb/Homo_erectus_new.JPG/300px-Homo_erectus_new.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cb/Homo_erectus_new.JPG/400px-Homo_erectus_new.JPG 2x" width="200"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Homo_erectus_new.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Reconstruction of <i>Homo erectus</i> (Westf&#228;lisches Landesmuseum, Herne, Germany, 2006).</div></div></div>\n<p>The <a href="/wiki/Homo" title="Homo">earliest humans</a> developed out of <a href="/wiki/Australopithecine" title="Australopithecine">australopithecine</a> ancestors after about 3 million years ago, most likely in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Eastern_Africa" title="Eastern Africa">Eastern Africa</a>, most likely in the area of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kenyan_Rift_Valley" title="Kenyan Rift Valley">Kenyan Rift Valley</a>, where the <a href="/wiki/Lomekwi" title="Lomekwi">oldest known stone tools</a> were found. Stone tools recently discovered at the <a href="/wiki/Shangchen" title="Shangchen">Shangchen</a> site in China and dated to 2.12 million years ago are claimed to be the earliest known evidence of hominins outside Africa, surpassing Dmanisi in Georgia by 300,000 years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3"><a href="#cite_note-3">[3]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Homo_erectus"><i>Homo erectus</i></span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Early_human_migrations&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Homo erectus">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Homo_erectus" title="Homo erectus">Homo erectus</a> and <a href="/wiki/Oldowan" title="Oldowan">Oldowan</a></div>\n<p>Between 3 and 2 million years ago, <i><a href="/wiki/Homo" title="Homo">Homo</a></i> spread throughout East Africa and to <a href="/wiki/Southern_Africa" title="Southern Africa">Southern Africa</a> (<i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Telanthropus_capensis" title="Telanthropus capensis">Telanthropus capensis</a></i>), but not yet to West Africa.\nAround 1.9 million years ago, <i><a href="/wiki/Homo_erectus" title="Homo erectus">Homo erectus</a></i> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Out_of_Africa_I" title="Out of Africa I">migrated out of Africa</a>  via the <a href="/wiki/Levantine_corridor" title="Levantine corridor">Levantine corridor</a> and <a href="/wiki/Horn_of_Africa" title="Horn of Africa">Horn of Africa</a> to <a href="/wiki/Eurasia" title="Eurasia">Eurasia</a>.\nThis migration has been proposed as being related to the  operation of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sahara_Pump_Theory" title="Sahara Pump Theory">Saharan pump</a>, around 1.9 million years ago.\n<i>Homo erectus</i> dispersed throughout most of the <a href="/wiki/Old_World" title="Old World">Old World</a>, reaching as far as <a href="/wiki/Southeast_Asia" title="Southeast Asia">Southeast Asia</a>.\nIts distribution is traced by the <a href="/wiki/Oldowan" title="Oldowan">Oldowan</a> lithic industry, by 1.3 million years ago extending as far north as the <a href="/wiki/40th_parallel_north" title="40th parallel north">40th parallel</a> (<a href="/wiki/Xiaochangliang" title="Xiaochangliang">Xiaochangliang</a>).\n</p><p>Key sites for this early migration out of Africa are <a href="/wiki/Riwat" title="Riwat">Riwat</a> in Pakistan (~2 Ma?<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4"><a href="#cite_note-4">[4]</a></sup>), <a href="/wiki/Ubeidiya" title="Ubeidiya">Ubeidiya</a> in the Levant (1.5 Ma) and <a href="/wiki/Dmanisi" title="Dmanisi">Dmanisi</a> in the Caucasus (1.81 &#177; 0.03 Ma, <a href="/wiki/P-value" title="P-value">p</a> = 0.05<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5"><a href="#cite_note-5">[5]</a></sup>).\n</p><p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Prehistoric_China" title="Prehistoric China">China</a> was populated as early as 1.66 Mya based on stone artifacts found in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nihewan_Basin" title="Nihewan Basin">Nihewan Basin</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup> The archaeological site of <a href="/wiki/Xihoudu" title="Xihoudu">Xihoudu</a> (<span lang="zh-Hant" title="Chinese language text">&#35199;&#20399;&#28193;</span>) in <a href="/wiki/Shanxi" title="Shanxi">Shanxi</a> Province is the earliest recorded <a href="/wiki/Control_of_fire_by_early_humans" title="Control of fire by early humans">use of fire</a> by <i>Homo erectus</i>, which is dated 1.27 million years ago.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MagnetostratigraphicDating_7-0"><a href="#cite_note-MagnetostratigraphicDating-7">[7]</a></sup>\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/Southeast_Asia" title="Southeast Asia">Southeast Asia</a> (<a href="/wiki/Java" title="Java">Java</a>) was reached about 1.7 million years ago (<a href="/wiki/Meganthropus" title="Meganthropus">Meganthropus</a>). Western  <a href="/wiki/Paleolithic_Europe" title="Paleolithic Europe">Europe</a> was first populated around 1.2 million years ago (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Archaeological_Site_of_Atapuerca" title="Archaeological Site of Atapuerca">Atapuerca</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Robert G. Bednarik has suggested that <i>Homo erectus</i> may have built rafts and sailed oceans, a theory that has raised some controversy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="After_H._erectus">After <i>H. erectus</i></span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Early_human_migrations&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: After H. erectus">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Clactonian" title="Clactonian">Clactonian</a>, <a href="/wiki/Micoquien" title="Micoquien">Micoquien</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Mousterian" title="Mousterian">Mousterian</a></div>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Neanderthal_extinction" title="Neanderthal extinction">Neanderthal extinction</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Spread_and_evolution_of_Denisovans.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="720" data-file-width="1280" decoding="async" height="124" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a6/Spread_and_evolution_of_Denisovans.jpg/220px-Spread_and_evolution_of_Denisovans.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a6/Spread_and_evolution_of_Denisovans.jpg/330px-Spread_and_evolution_of_Denisovans.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a6/Spread_and_evolution_of_Denisovans.jpg/440px-Spread_and_evolution_of_Denisovans.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Spread_and_evolution_of_Denisovans.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Spread of Denisovans and Neanderthals after 500,000 years ago</div></div></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Range_of_NeanderthalsAColoured.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="463" data-file-width="970" decoding="async" height="105" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d4/Range_of_NeanderthalsAColoured.png/220px-Range_of_NeanderthalsAColoured.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d4/Range_of_NeanderthalsAColoured.png/330px-Range_of_NeanderthalsAColoured.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d4/Range_of_NeanderthalsAColoured.png/440px-Range_of_NeanderthalsAColoured.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Range_of_NeanderthalsAColoured.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Known Neanderthal range with separate populations in  Europe and the Caucasus (blue), the Near East (orange), Uzbekistan (green), and the Altai region (purple)</div></div></div>\n<p>One million years after its dispersal, <i>H. erectus</i> was diverging into new species. <i>H. erectus</i> is a <a href="/wiki/Chronospecies" title="Chronospecies">chronospecies</a> and was never extinct, so that its "late survival" is a matter of taxonomic convention. Late forms of <i>H. erectus</i> are thought to have survived until after about 0.5 million ago to 143,000 years ago at the latest,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-extinction_10-0"><a href="#cite_note-extinction-10">[10]</a></sup> with derived forms classified as  <i><a href="/wiki/Homo_antecessor" title="Homo antecessor">H. antecessor</a></i> in Europe around 800,000 years ago\nand  <i><a href="/wiki/Homo_heidelbergensis" title="Homo heidelbergensis">H. heidelbergensis</a></i> in Africa around 600,000 years ago.\n<i>H. heidelbergensis</i> in its turn spreads across East Africa (<i><a href="/wiki/Homo_rhodesiensis" title="Homo rhodesiensis">H. rhodesiensis</a></i>) and to Eurasia, where it gives rise to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Neanderthals" title="Neanderthals">Neanderthals</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Denisovans" title="Denisovans">Denisovans</a>.\n</p><p><i>H. heidelbergensis</i>, Neanderthals and Denisovans expanded north beyond the <a href="/wiki/50th_parallel_north" title="50th parallel north">50th parallel</a> (<a href="/wiki/Eartham_Pit,_Boxgrove" title="Eartham Pit, Boxgrove">Eartham Pit, Boxgrove</a> 500kya, <a href="/wiki/Swanscombe_Heritage_Park" title="Swanscombe Heritage Park">Swanscombe Heritage Park</a> 400kya, <a href="/wiki/Denisova_Cave" title="Denisova Cave">Denisova Cave</a> 50 kya).\nIt has been suggested that late Neanderthals may even have reached the boundary of the <a href="/wiki/Arctic" title="Arctic">Arctic</a>, by c. 32,000 years ago,\nwhen they were being displaced from their earlier habitats by <i>H. sapiens</i>, based on 2011 excavations at the  site of Byzovaya in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Urals" title="Urals">Urals</a> (<a href="/wiki/Komi_Republic" title="Komi Republic">Komi Republic</a>, <span class="plainlinks nourlexpansion"><a class="external text" href="//tools.wmflabs.org/geohack/geohack.php?pagename=Early_human_migrations&amp;params=65.02_N_57.42_E_"><span class="geo-nondefault"><span class="geo-dms" title="Maps, aerial photos, and other data for this location"><span class="latitude">65&#176;01&#8242;N</span> <span class="longitude">57&#176;25&#8242;E</span></span></span><span class="geo-multi-punct">&#65279; / &#65279;</span><span class="geo-default"><span class="geo-dec" title="Maps, aerial photos, and other data for this location">65.02&#176;N 57.42&#176;E</span><span style="display:none">&#65279; / <span class="geo">65.02; 57.42</span></span></span></a></span>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Other archaic human species are assumed to have spread throughout Africa by this time, although the fossil record is sparse.\nTheir presence is assumed based on traces of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Archaic_human_admixture_with_modern_humans" title="Archaic human admixture with modern humans">admixture</a> with modern humans found in the genome of populations indigenous to Southern and West Africa.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hamgenev_14-0"><a href="#cite_note-hamgenev-14">[14]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lac12adaafr_16-0"><a href="#cite_note-lac12adaafr-16">[16]</a></sup>\n<i><a href="/wiki/Homo_naledi" title="Homo naledi">Homo naledi</a></i>, discovered in South Africa in 2013 and tentatively dated to about 300,000 years ago, may represent fossil evidence of such an archaic human species.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Neanderthals spread across the Near East and Europe, while Denisovans appear to have spread across Central and East Asia and to Southeast Asia and Oceania. There is evidence that Denisovans interbred with Neanderthals in Central Asia where their habitats overlapped.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lopezvan_Dorp2016_18-0"><a href="#cite_note-Lopezvan_Dorp2016-18">[18]</a></sup>\n</p><p>It is most likely from an African variety of <i>H. antecessor</i> that <i><a href="/wiki/Homo_sapiens" title="Homo sapiens">H. sapiens</a></i> develops around 300,000 years ago.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Homo_sapiens"><i>Homo sapiens</i></span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Early_human_migrations&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Homo sapiens">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:322px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Human_migrations_and_mitochondrial_haplogroups.PNG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="411" data-file-width="800" decoding="async" height="164" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/04/Human_migrations_and_mitochondrial_haplogroups.PNG/320px-Human_migrations_and_mitochondrial_haplogroups.PNG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/04/Human_migrations_and_mitochondrial_haplogroups.PNG/480px-Human_migrations_and_mitochondrial_haplogroups.PNG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/04/Human_migrations_and_mitochondrial_haplogroups.PNG/640px-Human_migrations_and_mitochondrial_haplogroups.PNG 2x" width="320"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Human_migrations_and_mitochondrial_haplogroups.PNG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Early modern human migrations based on the distribution of <a href="/wiki/Human_mitochondrial_DNA_haplogroup" title="Human mitochondrial DNA haplogroup">mitochondrial haplogroups</a>.</div></div></div>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Dispersal_throughout_Africa">Dispersal throughout Africa</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Early_human_migrations&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Dispersal throughout Africa">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Macro-haplogroup_L_(mtDNA)" title="Macro-haplogroup L (mtDNA)">Macro-haplogroup L (mtDNA)</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Archaeogenetics_of_sub-Saharan_Africa" title="Archaeogenetics of sub-Saharan Africa">Archaeogenetics of sub-Saharan Africa</a></div>\n<p><i><a href="/wiki/Homo_sapiens" title="Homo sapiens">Homo sapiens</a></i> (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anatomically_modern_humans" title="Anatomically modern humans">anatomically modern humans</a>) are assumed to have emerged about 300,000 years ago based on thermoluminescence dating of artefacts from <a href="/wiki/Jebel_Irhoud" title="Jebel Irhoud">Jebel Irhoud</a>, Morocco, published in 2017.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup>\nPreviously, the <a href="/wiki/Omo_remains" title="Omo remains">Omo remains</a>, excavated between 1967 and 1974 in <a href="/wiki/Omo_National_Park" title="Omo National Park">Omo National Park</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ethiopia" title="Ethiopia">Ethiopia</a>, and dated to 200,000 years ago, were long held to be the oldest known fossils of anatomically modern humans.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mcdougall2005_21-0"><a href="#cite_note-Mcdougall2005-21">[21]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Early modern humans expanded to Western Eurasia, Central, Western and Southern Africa from the time of their emergence. While <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Northern_Dispersal" title="Northern Dispersal">early expansions</a> to Eurasia appear not to have persisted,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hershkovitz2018_22-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hershkovitz2018-22">[22]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lopezvan_Dorp2016_18-1"><a href="#cite_note-Lopezvan_Dorp2016-18">[18]</a></sup> expansions to <a href="/wiki/Khoisan#Origins" title="Khoisan">Southern</a> and <a href="/wiki/African_Pygmies#Origins" title="African Pygmies">Central Africa</a> resulted in the deepest temporal divergence in living human populations.\nEarly modern human expansion in sub-Saharan Africa appears to contribute to the end of late <a href="/wiki/Acheulean" title="Acheulean">Acheulean</a> (<a href="/wiki/Fauresmith_(industry)" title="Fauresmith (industry)">Fauresmith</a>) industries at about 130,000 years ago, although very late coexistence of archaic and early modern humans, until as late as 12,000 years ago, has been argued for West Africa in particular.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Scerri2017_23-0"><a href="#cite_note-Scerri2017-23">[23]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The ancestors of the modern <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Khoi-San" title="Khoi-San">Khoi-San</a> expanded to Southern Africa before 150,000 years ago, possibly as early as before\n260,000 years ago,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Schlebusch2_24-0"><a href="#cite_note-Schlebusch2-24">[24]</a></sup> so that by the beginning of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/MIS_5" title="MIS 5">MIS 5</a> "<a href="/wiki/Megadrought" title="Megadrought">megadrought</a>", 130,000 years ago, there were two ancestral population clusters in Africa, bearers of <a href="/wiki/Haplogroup_L0_(mtDNA)" title="Haplogroup L0 (mtDNA)">mt-DNA haplogroup L0</a> in southern Africa, ancestral to the Khoi-San, and bearers of <a href="/wiki/Macro-haplogroup_L_(mtDNA)" title="Macro-haplogroup L (mtDNA)">haplogroup L1-6</a> in central/eastern Africa, ancestral to everyone else. There was a significant back-migration of bearers of L0 towards eastern Africa between 120 and 75 kya.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Expansion to Central Africa, by the ancestors of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Central_African_forager" title="Central African forager">Central African forager</a> populations (African Pygmies) most likely took place before 130,000 years ago, and certainly before 60,000 years ago.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The situation in <a href="/wiki/West_Africa" title="West Africa">West Africa</a> is difficult to interpret due to a sparsity of fossil evidence.  <i>Homo sapiens</i> seems to have reached the western <a href="/wiki/Sahel" title="Sahel">Sahelian zone</a> by 130 kya, while tropical West African sites associated with <i>H. sapiens</i> are known only from after 130 kya. Unlike elsewhere in Africa, archaic <a href="/wiki/Middle_Stone_Age" title="Middle Stone Age">MSA</a> sites appear to persist until very late, down to the Holocene boundary (12 kya), pointing to the possibility of late survival of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Archaic_human" title="Archaic human">archaic humans</a>, and late <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Archaic_admixture_to_anatomically_modern_humans" title="Archaic admixture to anatomically modern humans">hybridization</a> with <i>H. sapiens</i> in West Africa.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Scerri2017_23-1"><a href="#cite_note-Scerri2017-23">[23]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_northern_Africa_dispersal">Early northern Africa dispersal</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Early_human_migrations&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Early northern Africa dispersal">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Northern_Dispersal" title="Northern Dispersal">Northern Dispersal</a></div>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Sahara_pump_theory" title="Sahara pump theory">Sahara pump theory</a></div>\n<p>Populations of <i>H. sapiens</i> migrated to the Levant and to Europe between 130,000 and 115,000 years ago, and possibly in earlier waves as early as 185,000 years ago.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hershkovitz2018_22-1"><a href="#cite_note-Hershkovitz2018-22">[22]</a></sup>\nThese early migrations do not appear to have led to lasting colonisation and receded by about 80,000 years ago.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lopezvan_Dorp2016_18-2"><a href="#cite_note-Lopezvan_Dorp2016-18">[18]</a></sup>\nThere is a possibility that this first wave of expansion may have reached China (or even to North America<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Accuracy_dispute#Disputed_statement" title="Wikipedia:Accuracy dispute"><span title="The material near this tag is possibly inaccurate or nonfactual. (January 2018)">dubious</span></a> <span class="metadata"> &#8211; <a href="/wiki/Talk:Early_human_migrations#Dubious" title="Talk:Early human migrations">discuss</a></span></i>]</sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup>) as early as 125,000 years ago, but would have died out without leaving a trace in the genome of contemporary humans.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lopezvan_Dorp2016_18-3"><a href="#cite_note-Lopezvan_Dorp2016-18">[18]</a></sup>\n</p><p>There is some evidence for the argument that modern humans left Africa at least 125,000 years ago using two different routes: through the <a href="/wiki/Nile" title="Nile">Nile Valley</a> heading to the Middle East, at least into modern Israel (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Qafzeh" title="Qafzeh">Qafzeh</a>: 120,000&#8211;100,000 years ago); and a second one through the present-day <a href="/wiki/Bab-el-Mandeb" title="Bab-el-Mandeb">Bab-el-Mandeb</a> Strait on the Red Sea (at that time, with a much lower sea level and narrower extension), crossing it into the Arabian Peninsula, settling in places like the present-day United Arab Emirates (125,000 years ago)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup> and Oman (106,000 years ago)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup> and then possibly going into the Indian Subcontinent (<a href="/wiki/Jwalapuram" title="Jwalapuram">Jwalapuram</a>: 75,000 years ago). Despite the fact that no human remains have yet been found in these three places, the apparent similarities between the stone tools found at <a href="/wiki/Jebel_Faya" title="Jebel Faya">Jebel Faya</a>, the ones from Jwalapuram and some African ones suggest that their creators were all modern humans.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup> These findings might give some support to the claim that modern humans from Africa arrived at southern China about 100,000 years ago (<a href="/wiki/Zhiren_Cave" title="Zhiren Cave">Zhiren Cave</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Zhirendong" title="Zhirendong">Zhirendong</a>, <a href="/wiki/Chongzuo" title="Chongzuo">Chongzuo</a> City: 100,000 years ago;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup> and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Liujiang_hominid" title="Liujiang hominid">Liujiang hominid</a> (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Liujiang_County" title="Liujiang County">Liujiang County</a>): controversially dated at 139,000&#8211;111,000 years ago <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup>). Dating results of the <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Lunadong&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Lunadong (page does not exist)">Lunadong</a> (<a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Bubing_Basin&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Bubing Basin (page does not exist)">Bubing Basin</a>, <a href="/wiki/Guangxi" title="Guangxi">Guangxi</a>, southern China) teeth, which include a right upper second molar and a left lower second molar, indicate that the molars may be as old as 126,000 years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Since these previous exits from Africa did not leave traces in the results of genetic analyses based on the Y chromosome and on MtDNA (which represent only a small part of the human genetic material), it seems that those modern humans did not survive or survived in small numbers and were assimilated by our major antecessors. An explanation for their extinction (or small genetic imprint) may be the <a href="/wiki/Toba_catastrophe_theory" title="Toba catastrophe theory">Toba catastrophe theory</a> (74,000 years ago). However, some argue that its impact on the human population was not dramatic.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup>\n</p><p>An <a href="/wiki/Asia" title="Asia">Asia</a> center of origin and dispersal for the mtDNA <a href="/wiki/Haplogroup_L3_(mtDNA)" title="Haplogroup L3 (mtDNA)">haplogroup L3</a> has also been hypothesized based on the fossil record, the similar coalescence dates of L3 and its Eurasian-distributed <a href="/wiki/Haplogroup_M_(mtDNA)" title="Haplogroup M (mtDNA)">M</a> and <a href="/wiki/Haplogroup_N_(mtDNA)" title="Haplogroup N (mtDNA)">N</a> derivative clades (~71 kya), the distant location in <a href="/wiki/Southeast_Asia" title="Southeast Asia">Southeast Asia</a> of the oldest subclades of M and N, and the comparable age of the paternal haplogroup <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Haplogroup_DE_(Y-DNA)" title="Haplogroup DE (Y-DNA)">DE</a>. After an initial Out-of-Africa migration of early <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anatomically_modern_human" title="Anatomically modern human">anatomically modern humans</a> around 125 kya, fully modern human L3-carrying females are thus proposed to have back-migrated from the maternal haplogroup\'s place of origin in Eurasia around 70 kya along with males bearing the paternal haplogroup <a href="/wiki/Haplogroup_E-M96" title="Haplogroup E-M96">E</a>, which is also thought to have originated in Eurasia. These new Eurasian lineages are then suggested to have largely replaced the old autochthonous male and female African lineages.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Coastal_migration">Coastal migration</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Early_human_migrations&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Coastal migration">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Coastal_migration" title="Coastal migration">Coastal migration</a></div>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Recent_African_origin_of_modern_humans" title="Recent African origin of modern humans">Recent African origin of modern humans</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Archaic_human_admixture_with_modern_humans" title="Archaic human admixture with modern humans">Archaic human admixture with modern humans</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Archaic_humans_in_Southeast_Asia#Anatomically_modern_humans_in_southeast_Asia" title="Archaic humans in Southeast Asia">Archaic humans in Southeast Asia &#167;&#160;Anatomically modern humans in southeast Asia</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:352px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Early_migrations_mercator.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="569" data-file-width="1173" decoding="async" height="170" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8b/Early_migrations_mercator.svg/350px-Early_migrations_mercator.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8b/Early_migrations_mercator.svg/525px-Early_migrations_mercator.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8b/Early_migrations_mercator.svg/700px-Early_migrations_mercator.svg.png 2x" width="350"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Early_migrations_mercator.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Overview map of the peopling of the world by early humans during the <a href="/wiki/Upper_Paleolithic" title="Upper Paleolithic">Upper Paleolithic</a>, following to the <a href="/wiki/Southern_Dispersal" title="Southern Dispersal">Southern Dispersal</a> paradigm.</div></div></div>\n<p>The so-called "<a href="/wiki/Recent_African_origin_of_modern_humans#Second_dispersal_-_Southern_Route_dispersal" title="Recent African origin of modern humans">recent dispersal</a>" of modern humans has taken place after beginning about 70,000 years ago.  It is this migration wave that led to the lasting spread of modern humans throughout the world.\n</p><p>A small group of members of a population inhabiting East Africa, who were bearers of the mitochondrial haplogroup <a href="/wiki/Haplogroup_L3_(mtDNA)" title="Haplogroup L3 (mtDNA)">L3</a> and numbered\npossibly fewer than 1,000 individuals,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup> crossed the <a href="/wiki/Red_Sea" title="Red Sea">Red Sea</a> strait at <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bab_el_Mandib" title="Bab el Mandib">Bab el Mandib</a>, to what is now <a href="/wiki/Yemen" title="Yemen">Yemen</a>, after around 75,000 years ago.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup> A recent review has shown support for both the Northern Route through Sinai/Israel/Syria (Levant), and, that both routes may have been used.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lopezvan_Dorp2016_18-4"><a href="#cite_note-Lopezvan_Dorp2016-18">[18]</a></sup>\nTheir descendants spread along the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Coastal_migration" title="Coastal migration">coastal route</a> around <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Arabia" title="Arabia">Arabia</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Persia" title="Persia">Persia</a> to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Paleolithic_South_Asia" title="Paleolithic South Asia">Indian subcontinent</a> before 55,000 years ago.\nThe coastal migration scenario between roughly 70,000 and 50,000 years ago is associated with mitochondrial haplogroups\n<a href="/wiki/Haplogroup_M_(mtDNA)" title="Haplogroup M (mtDNA)">M</a> and <a href="/wiki/Haplogroup_N_(mtDNA)" title="Haplogroup N (mtDNA)">N</a>, both derivative of L3.\n</p><p>A fragment of a jawbone with eight teeth found at <a href="/wiki/Archaeology_of_Israel#Carmel_Caves" title="Archaeology of Israel">Misliya Cave</a>, Israel, have been dated to around 185,000 years ago. Layers dating from between 250,000 and 140,000 years ago in the same cave contained tools of the <a href="/wiki/Levallois_technique" title="Levallois technique">Levallois</a> type which could put the date of the first migration even earlier if the tools can be associated with the modern human jawbone finds.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_40-0"><a href="#cite_note-:0-40">[40]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_41-0"><a href="#cite_note-:1-41">[41]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Along the way <i>H. sapiens</i> interbred with Neanderthals and Denisovans,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup> with Denisovan DNA making 0.2% of mainland Asian and Native American DNA.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NAT-2013_43-0"><a href="#cite_note-NAT-2013-43">[43]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Oceania">Oceania</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Early_human_migrations&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Oceania">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<p>Migrations continued along the Asian coast to Southeast Asia and Oceania, colonising <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Prehistoric_Australia" title="Prehistoric Australia">Australia</a> before 50,000 years ago.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bowler_44-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bowler-44">[44]</a></sup>\nBy reaching Australia, <i>H. sapiens</i> for the first time expanded its habitat beyond that of <i>H. erectus</i>.\n</p><p>Denisovan ancestry is shared by Melanesians, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Australian_Aborigines" title="Australian Aborigines">Australian Aborigines</a>, and smaller scattered groups of people in Southeast Asia, such as the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mamanwa" title="Mamanwa">Mamanwa</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Negrito" title="Negrito">Negrito</a> people in the <a href="/wiki/Philippines" title="Philippines">Philippines</a> suggesting the interbreeding took place in Eastern Asia where the Denisovans lived.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Callaway._45-0"><a href="#cite_note-Callaway.-45">[45]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Reich_46-0"><a href="#cite_note-Reich-46">[46]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Choi._47-0"><a href="#cite_note-Choi.-47">[47]</a></sup> Denisovans may have crossed the <a href="/wiki/Wallace_Line" title="Wallace Line">Wallace Line</a>, with <a href="/wiki/Wallacea" title="Wallacea">Wallacea</a> serving as their last <a href="/wiki/Refugium_(population_biology)" title="Refugium (population biology)">refugium</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cooper_48-0"><a href="#cite_note-Cooper-48">[48]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup> Homo erectus crossed the Lombok gap reaching as far as Flores, but never made it to Australia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Map_of_Sunda_and_Sahul.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1007" data-file-width="1111" decoding="async" height="227" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5f/Map_of_Sunda_and_Sahul.png/250px-Map_of_Sunda_and_Sahul.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5f/Map_of_Sunda_and_Sahul.png/375px-Map_of_Sunda_and_Sahul.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5f/Map_of_Sunda_and_Sahul.png/500px-Map_of_Sunda_and_Sahul.png 2x" width="250"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Map_of_Sunda_and_Sahul.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The map shows the probable extent of land and water at the time of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Last_glacial_maximum" title="Last glacial maximum">last glacial maximum</a>, 20,000 yrs ago and when the sea level was probably more than 110m lower than today.</div></div></div>\n<p>During this time sea level was much lower and most of <a href="/wiki/Maritime_Southeast_Asia" title="Maritime Southeast Asia">Maritime Southeast Asia</a> formed one land mass known as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sunda_shelf" title="Sunda shelf">Sunda</a>. Migration continued Southeast on the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Great_Coastal_Migration" title="Great Coastal Migration">coastal route</a> to the <a href="/wiki/Strait" title="Strait">straits</a> between Sunda and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sahul_continent" title="Sahul continent">Sahul</a>, the continental land mass of present-day Australia and <a href="/wiki/New_Guinea" title="New Guinea">New Guinea</a>. The gaps on the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Weber_Line" title="Weber Line">Weber Line</a> are up to 90&#160;km wide,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup> so the migration to Australia and New Guinea would have required seafaring skills. Migration also continued along the coast eventually turning northeast to <a href="/wiki/China" title="China">China</a> and finally reaching <a href="/wiki/Japan" title="Japan">Japan</a> before turning inland. This is evidenced by the pattern of <a href="/wiki/Human_mitochondrial_DNA_haplogroup" title="Human mitochondrial DNA haplogroup">mitochondrial haplogroups</a> descended from <a href="/wiki/Haplogroup_M_(mtDNA)" title="Haplogroup M (mtDNA)">haplogroup M</a>, and in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Human_Y-chromosome_DNA_haplogroups" title="Human Y-chromosome DNA haplogroups">Y-chromosome</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Haplogroup_C_(Y-DNA)" title="Haplogroup C (Y-DNA)">haplogroup C</a>.\n</p><p>Sequencing of one Aboriginal genome from an old hair sample in Western Australia, revealed that the individual was descended from people who migrated into East Asia between 62,000 and 75,000 years ago. This supports the theory of a single migration into Australia and New Guinea before the arrival of Modern Asians (between 25,000 and 38,000 years ago) and their later migration into North America.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup> This migration is believed to have happened around 50,000 years ago, before Australia and New Guinea were separated by rising sea levels approximately 8,000 years ago.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup> This is supported by a date of 50,000&#8211;60,000 years ago for the oldest evidence of settlement in Australia,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bowler_44-1"><a href="#cite_note-Bowler-44">[44]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[55]</a></sup> around 40,000 years ago for the oldest human remains,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bowler_44-2"><a href="#cite_note-Bowler-44">[44]</a></sup> the earliest humans artifacts which are at least 65,000 years old<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup> and the extinction of the <a href="/wiki/Australian_megafauna" title="Australian megafauna">Australian megafauna</a> by humans between 46,000 and 15,000 years ago argued by Tim Flannery,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[57]</a></sup> which is similar to what happened in the Americas. The continued use of stone age tools in Australia has been much debated.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[58]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Dispersal_throughout_Eurasia">Dispersal throughout Eurasia</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Early_human_migrations&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Dispersal throughout Eurasia">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Upper_Paleolithic" title="Upper Paleolithic">Upper Paleolithic</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mammoth_steppe" title="Mammoth steppe">Mammoth steppe</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Archaic_human_admixture_with_modern_humans" title="Archaic human admixture with modern humans">Archaic human admixture with modern humans</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Mousterian" title="Mousterian">Mousterian</a></div>\n<p>The population brought to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Paleolithic_South_Asia" title="Paleolithic South Asia">South Asia</a> by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Coastal_migration" title="Coastal migration">coastal migration</a> appears to have remained there for some time, during roughly 60,000 to 50,000 years ago, before spreading further throughout Eurasia.\nThis dispersal of early humans, at the beginning of the <a href="/wiki/Upper_Paleolithic" title="Upper Paleolithic">Upper Paleolithic</a>, gave rise to the major population groups of the <a href="/wiki/Old_World" title="Old World">Old World</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Paleo-Indians" title="Paleo-Indians">Americas</a>.\n</p><p>Towards the West, Upper Paleolithic populations associated with mitochondrial haplogroup <a href="/wiki/Haplogroup_R_(mtDNA)" title="Haplogroup R (mtDNA)">R</a> and its derivatives, spread throughout Asia and Europe, with a back-migration of <a href="/wiki/Haplogroup_M_(mtDNA)#Haplogroup_M1" title="Haplogroup M (mtDNA)">M1</a> to North Africa and the Horn of Africa several millennia ago.\n</p><p>Presence <a href="/wiki/European_early_modern_humans" title="European early modern humans">in Europe</a> is certain after 40,000 years ago, possibly as early as 43,000 years ago,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[59]</a></sup>\nrapidly replacing the Neanderthal population.\nContemporary Europeans have <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Archaic_human_admixture_with_modern_humans" title="Archaic human admixture with modern humans">Neanderthal ancestry</a>,\nbut it seems likely that substantial interbreeding with Neanderthals ceased before 47,000 years ago, i.e. took place before modern humans entered Europe.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sank12datein_60-0"><a href="#cite_note-sank12datein-60">[60]</a></sup>\n</p><p>There is evidence from <a href="/wiki/Mitochondrial_DNA" title="Mitochondrial DNA">mitochondrial DNA</a> that modern humans have passed through at least one <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Genetic_bottleneck" title="Genetic bottleneck">genetic bottleneck</a>, in which genome diversity was drastically reduced.  <a href="/wiki/Henry_Harpending" title="Henry Harpending">Henry Harpending</a> has proposed that humans spread from a geographically restricted area about 100,000 years ago, the passage through the geographic bottleneck and then with a dramatic growth amongst geographically dispersed populations about 50,000 years ago, beginning first in Africa and thence spreading elsewhere.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Harpending_61-0"><a href="#cite_note-Harpending-61">[61]</a></sup> \nClimatological and geological evidence suggests evidence for the bottleneck.  The explosion of <a href="/wiki/Lake_Toba" title="Lake Toba">Lake Toba</a> created a 1,000 year cold period, as a result of the largest volcanic eruption of the <a href="/wiki/Quaternary" title="Quaternary">Quaternary</a>, potentially reducing human populations to a few tropical refugia.  It has been estimated that as few as 15,000 humans survived.  In such circumstances genetic drift and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Founder_effects" title="Founder effects">founder effects</a> may have been maximised.  The greater diversity amongst African genomes may be in part due to the greater prevalence of African refugia during the Toba incident.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup> However, a recent review highlights that the single-source hypothesis of non-African populations is less supported by ancient DNA analysis than multiple sources plus genetic mixing across Eurasia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lopezvan_Dorp2016_18-5"><a href="#cite_note-Lopezvan_Dorp2016-18">[18]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Europe">Europe</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Early_human_migrations&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Europe">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/European_early_modern_humans" title="European early modern humans">European early modern humans</a>, <a href="/wiki/Aurignacian" title="Aurignacian">Aurignacian</a>, <a href="/wiki/Gravettian" title="Gravettian">Gravettian</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Art_of_the_Upper_Paleolithic" title="Art of the Upper Paleolithic">Art of the Upper Paleolithic</a></div>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Hominid_dispersals_in_Europe" title="Hominid dispersals in Europe">Hominid dispersals in Europe</a></div>\n<p>The recent expansion of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anatomically_modern_humans" title="Anatomically modern humans">anatomically modern humans</a> reached Europe around 40,000 years ago, from Central Asia and the Middle East, as a result of cultural adaption to big game hunting of sub-glacial steppe fauna.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[63]</a></sup>\n<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Neanderthals" title="Neanderthals">Neanderthals</a> were present both in the Middle East and in Europe, and the arriving populations of anatomically modern humans (also known as "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cro-Magnon" title="Cro-Magnon">Cro-Magnon</a>" or <a href="/wiki/European_early_modern_humans" title="European early modern humans">European early modern humans</a>)\nhave <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Archaic_human_admixture_with_modern_humans" title="Archaic human admixture with modern humans">interbred with Neanderthal populations</a> to a limited degree.\nPopulations of modern humans and Neanderthal overlapped in various regions such as in Iberian peninsula and in the Middle East. Interbreeding may have contributed Neanderthal genes to palaeolithic and ultimately modern Eurasians and Oceanians.\n</p><p>An important difference between Europe and other parts of the inhabited world was the northern latitude. Archaeological evidence suggests humans, whether Neanderthal or Cro-Magnon, reached <a href="/wiki/Mamontovaya_Kurya" title="Mamontovaya Kurya">sites in Arctic Russia</a> by 40,000 years ago.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[64]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Cro-Magnon are considered the first anatomically modern humans in Europe. They entered <a href="/wiki/Eurasia" title="Eurasia">Eurasia</a> by the <a href="/wiki/Zagros_Mountains" title="Zagros Mountains">Zagros Mountains</a> (near present-day <a href="/wiki/Iran" title="Iran">Iran</a> and eastern <a href="/wiki/Turkey" title="Turkey">Turkey</a>) around 50,000 years ago, with one group rapidly settling coastal areas around the <a href="/wiki/Indian_Ocean" title="Indian Ocean">Indian Ocean</a> and one group migrating north to steppes of <a href="/wiki/Central_Asia" title="Central Asia">Central Asia</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Genographic_65-0"><a href="#cite_note-Genographic-65">[65]</a></sup> Modern human remains dating to 43&#8211;45,000 years ago have been discovered in Italy<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[66]</a></sup> and Britain,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[67]</a></sup> with the remains found of those that reached the European Russian Arctic 40,000 years ago.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[68]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[69]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Humans colonised the environment west of the Urals, hunting reindeer especially,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hoffecker,_J_2006_70-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hoffecker,_J_2006-70">[70]</a></sup> but were faced with adaptive challenges; winter temperatures averaged from &#8722;20 to &#8722;30&#160;&#176;C (&#8722;4 to &#8722;22&#160;&#176;F)  while fuel and shelter were scarce. They travelled on foot and relied on hunting highly mobile herds for food. These challenges were overcome through technological innovations: production of tailored clothing from the pelts of fur-bearing animals; construction of  shelters with hearths using bones as fuel; and digging of &#8220;ice cellars&#8221; into the permafrost for storing meat and bones.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hoffecker,_J_2006_70-1"><a href="#cite_note-Hoffecker,_J_2006-70">[70]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[71]</a></sup>\n</p><p>A <a href="/wiki/Mitochondrial_DNA" title="Mitochondrial DNA">mitochondrial DNA</a> sequence of two Cro-Magnons from the <a href="/wiki/Paglicci_Cave" title="Paglicci Cave">Paglicci Cave</a> in Italy, dated to 23,000 and 24,000 years old (Paglicci 52 and 12), identified the <a href="/wiki/Mitochondrial_DNA" title="Mitochondrial DNA">mtDNA</a> as <a href="/wiki/Haplogroup_N_(mtDNA)" title="Haplogroup N (mtDNA)">Haplogroup N</a>, typical of the latter group.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[72]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r856303569">@media all and (max-width:720px){.mw-parser-output .tmulti>.thumbinner{width:100%!important;max-width:none!important}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .tsingle{float:none!important;max-width:none!important;width:100%!important;text-align:center}}</style><div class="thumb tmulti tnone center"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:696px;max-width:696px"><div style="clear:both;font-weight:bold;text-align:center;background-color:transparent"><b>Migration of modern humans into Europe, based on simulation by Currat &amp; Excoffier (2004)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Currat_73-0"><a href="#cite_note-Currat-73">[73]</a></sup><br/></b>\n(YBP=Years before present)</div><div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:172px;max-width:172px"><div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Cro-Magnon_range_37,500_ybp.svg"><img
