srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f8/Cro-Magnon_range_32%2C500_ybp.svg/255px-Cro-Magnon_range_32%2C500_ybp.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f8/Cro-Magnon_range_32%2C500_ybp.svg/340px-Cro-Magnon_range_32%2C500_ybp.svg.png 2x" width="170"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left"><b>Up to 32,500 YBP</b></div></div><div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:172px;max-width:172px"><div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Cro-Magnon_range_30,000_ybp.svg"><img alt="" data-file-height="130" data-file-width="147" decoding="async" height="150" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/13/Cro-Magnon_range_30%2C000_ybp.svg/170px-Cro-Magnon_range_30%2C000_ybp.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/13/Cro-Magnon_range_30%2C000_ybp.svg/255px-Cro-Magnon_range_30%2C000_ybp.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/13/Cro-Magnon_range_30%2C000_ybp.svg/340px-Cro-Magnon_range_30%2C000_ybp.svg.png 2x" width="170"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left"><b>Up to 30,000 YBP</b></div></div><div style="clear:left"></div></div></div>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Neanderthal_extinction_hypotheses" title="Neanderthal extinction hypotheses">Neanderthal extinction hypotheses</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Archaic_human_admixture_with_modern_humans" title="Archaic human admixture with modern humans">Archaic human admixture with modern humans</a></div>\n<p>The expansion of modern human population is thought to have begun 45,000 years ago, and may have taken 15,000&#8211;20,000 years for Europe to be colonized.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Maca01_74-0"><a href="#cite_note-Maca01-74">[74]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[75]</a></sup>\n</p><p>During this time the Neanderthals were slowly being displaced. Because it took so long for Europe to be occupied, it appears that humans and Neanderthals may have been constantly competing for territory. The Neanderthals had larger brains, and were larger overall, with a more robust or heavily built frame, which suggests that they were physically stronger than modern <i>Homo sapiens</i>. Having lived in Europe for 200,000 years, they would have been better adapted to the cold weather. The anatomically modern humans known as the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cro-Magnons" title="Cro-Magnons">Cro-Magnons</a>, with widespread trade networks, superior technology and bodies likely better suited to running, would eventually completely displace the Neanderthals, whose last refuge was in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Iberian_peninsula" title="Iberian peninsula">Iberian peninsula</a>. After about 25,000 years ago the fossil record of the Neanderthals ends, indicating that they had become extinct. The last known population lived around a cave system on the remote south-facing coast of <a href="/wiki/Gibraltar" title="Gibraltar">Gibraltar</a> from 30,000 to 24,000 years ago.\n</p><p>From the extent of linkage disequilibrium, it was estimated that the last Neanderthal gene flow into early ancestors of Europeans occurred 47,000&#8211;65,000 years <a href="/wiki/Before_Present" title="Before Present">BP</a>. In conjunction with archaeological and fossil evidence, the gene flow is thought likely to have occurred somewhere in Western Eurasia, possibly the Middle East.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sank12datein_60-1"><a href="#cite_note-sank12datein-60">[60]</a></sup> Studies show a higher Neanderthal admixture in East Asians than in Europeans.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-meyer12highcov_76-0"><a href="#cite_note-meyer12highcov-76">[76]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wall13higher_77-0"><a href="#cite_note-wall13higher-77">[77]</a></sup> North African groups share a similar excess of derived alleles with Neanderthals as do non-African populations, whereas Sub-Saharan African groups are the only modern human populations that generally did not experience Neanderthal admixture.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Quinto2012-b_78-0"><a href="#cite_note-Quinto2012-b-78">[78]</a></sup> The Neanderthal-linked haplotype B006 of the dystrophin gene has also been found among nomad pastoralist groups in the Sahel and Horn of Africa, who are associated with northern populations. Consequently, the presence of this B006 haplotype on the northern and northeastern perimeter of Sub-Saharan Africa is attributed to gene flow from a non-African point of origin.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79"><a href="#cite_note-79">[79]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="East_and_North_Asia">East and North Asia</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Early_human_migrations&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: East and North Asia">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Mongoloid#Genetic_research" title="Mongoloid">Mongoloid &#167;&#160;Genetic_research</a></div>\n<p>"<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tianyuan_Man" title="Tianyuan Man">Tianyuan Man</a>", an individual who lived in China c. 40,000 years ago, showed substantial Neanderthal admixture.\nA 2017 study of the ancient DNA of Tianyuan Man found that the individual is closely related to modern East Asian populations, but not a direct ancestor.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80"><a href="#cite_note-80">[80]</a></sup>\nA 2013 study found <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Neanderthal_admixture" title="Neanderthal admixture">Neanderthal introgression</a> of 18 genes within the chromosome 3p21.31 region (HYAL region) of East Asians.  The introgressive haplotypes were positively selected in only East Asian populations, rising steadily from 45,000 years ago until a sudden increase of growth rate around 5,000 to 3,500 years ago. They occur at very high frequencies among East Asian populations in contrast to other Eurasian populations (e.g. European and South Asian populations). The findings also suggests that this Neanderthal introgression occurred within the ancestral population shared by East Asians and Native Americans.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dinch3_81-0"><a href="#cite_note-dinch3-81">[81]</a></sup>\n</p><p>A 2016 study presented an analysis of the population genetics of the <a href="/wiki/Ainu_people" title="Ainu people">Ainu</a> people of northern Japan as key to the reconstruction of the early peopling of East Asia. The Ainu were found to represent a more basal branch than the modern farming populations of East Asia, suggesting an ancient (pre-Neolithic) connection with northeast Siberians.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82"><a href="#cite_note-82">[82]</a></sup>\nA 2013 study associated several phenotypical traits associated with Mongoloids with a single mutation of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/EDAR" title="EDAR">EDAR</a> gene, dated to c. 35,000 years ago.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83"><a href="#cite_note-83">[83]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ancient_North_Eurasians" title="Ancient North Eurasians">Ancient North Eurasians</a></div>\n<p>Mitochondrial haplogroups <a href="/wiki/Haplogroup_A_(mtDNA)" title="Haplogroup A (mtDNA)">A</a>, <a href="/wiki/Haplogroup_B_(mtDNA)" title="Haplogroup B (mtDNA)">B</a> and <a href="/wiki/Haplogroup_G_(mtDNA)" title="Haplogroup G (mtDNA)">G</a> originated about 50,000 years ago, and bearers subsequently colonized <a href="/wiki/Siberia" title="Siberia">Siberia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Prehistoric_Korea" title="Prehistoric Korea">Korea</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Prehistoric_Japan" title="Prehistoric Japan">Japan</a>, by about 35,000 years ago. Parts of these populations migrated to North America during the <a href="/wiki/Last_Glacial_Maximum" title="Last Glacial Maximum">Last Glacial Maximum</a>.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Last_Glacial_Maximum">Last Glacial Maximum</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Early_human_migrations&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Last Glacial Maximum">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Eurasia">Eurasia</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Early_human_migrations&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Eurasia">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Solutrean" title="Solutrean">Solutrean</a> and <a href="/wiki/Magdalenian" title="Magdalenian">Magdalenian</a></div>\n<p>Around 20,000 years ago, approximately 5,000 years after the Neanderthal extinction, the <a href="/wiki/Last_Glacial_Maximum" title="Last Glacial Maximum">Last Glacial Maximum</a> forced northern hemisphere inhabitants to migrate to several <a href="/wiki/Last_Glacial_Maximum_refugia" title="Last Glacial Maximum refugia">shelters</a> (known as <a href="/wiki/Refugium_(population_biology)" title="Refugium (population biology)">refugia</a>) until the end of this period. The resulting populations are then presumed to have resided in such refuges during the LGM to ultimately reoccupy Europe where archaic historical populations are considered their descendants.\nThe composition of European populations was later altered by further migrations, notably the  <a href="/wiki/Neolithic_Europe" title="Neolithic Europe">Neolithic</a> expansion from the Middle East, and still later the <a href="/wiki/Chalcolithic" title="Chalcolithic">Chalcolithic</a> population movements associated with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Indo-European_expansion" title="Indo-European expansion">Indo-European expansion</a>.\nA Paleolithic site on the Yana River, Siberia, at 71&#176;N, lies well above the Arctic Circle and dates to 27,000 radiocarbon years before present, during glacial times. This site shows that people adapted to this harsh, high-latitude, Late Pleistocene environment much earlier than previously thought.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84"><a href="#cite_note-84">[84]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The African <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Epipaleolithic" title="Epipaleolithic">Epipaleolithic</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kebaran_culture" title="Kebaran culture">Kebaran culture</a> is believed to have reached the Near East about 18,000 years ago, introducing the bow and arrow.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (January 2018)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Americas">Americas</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Early_human_migrations&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Americas">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Settlement_of_the_Americas" title="Settlement of the Americas">Settlement of the Americas</a> and <a href="/wiki/Genetic_history_of_indigenous_peoples_of_the_Americas" title="Genetic history of indigenous peoples of the Americas">Genetic history of indigenous peoples of the Americas</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Map_of_gene_flow_in_and_out_of_Beringia.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="884" data-file-width="708" decoding="async" height="212" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/67/Map_of_gene_flow_in_and_out_of_Beringia.jpg/170px-Map_of_gene_flow_in_and_out_of_Beringia.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/67/Map_of_gene_flow_in_and_out_of_Beringia.jpg/255px-Map_of_gene_flow_in_and_out_of_Beringia.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/67/Map_of_gene_flow_in_and_out_of_Beringia.jpg/340px-Map_of_gene_flow_in_and_out_of_Beringia.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Map_of_gene_flow_in_and_out_of_Beringia.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Schematic illustration of the <a href="/wiki/Beringia" title="Beringia">Beringia</a> migration based on <a href="/wiki/Human_mitochondrial_DNA_haplogroup" title="Human mitochondrial DNA haplogroup">matrilineal genetics</a>: Arrival of Central Asian populations to the Beringian <a href="/wiki/Mammoth_steppe" title="Mammoth steppe">Mammoth steppe</a> c. 25,000 years ago, followed by a "swift peoplling of the Americas"<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (July 2018)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> c. 15,000 years ago.</div></div></div>\n<p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Paleo-Indian" title="Paleo-Indian">Paleo-Indians</a> originated from <a href="/wiki/Central_Asia" title="Central Asia">Central Asia</a>, crossing the <a href="/wiki/Beringia" title="Beringia">Beringia land bridge</a> between eastern Siberia and present-day Alaska.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SpencerWells2_85-0"><a href="#cite_note-SpencerWells2-85">[85]</a></sup> Humans lived throughout the Americas by the end of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Last_glacial_period" title="Last glacial period">last glacial period</a>, or more specifically what is known as the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Late_Glacial_Maximum#North_America" title="Late Glacial Maximum">late glacial maximum</a>, no earlier than 23,000 years before present.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SpencerWells2_85-1"><a href="#cite_note-SpencerWells2-85">[85]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Smithsonian_86-0"><a href="#cite_note-Smithsonian-86">[86]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87"><a href="#cite_note-87">[87]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88"><a href="#cite_note-88">[88]</a></sup> Details of Paleo-Indian migration to and throughout the American continent, including the dates and the routes traveled, are subject to ongoing research and discussion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-national_89-0"><a href="#cite_note-national-89">[89]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The routes of migration are also debated. The traditional theory is that these early migrants moved when sea levels were significantly lowered due to the <a href="/wiki/Quaternary_glaciation" title="Quaternary glaciation">Quaternary glaciation</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Smithsonian_86-1"><a href="#cite_note-Smithsonian-86">[86]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-national_89-1"><a href="#cite_note-national-89">[89]</a></sup> following herds of now-extinct <a href="/wiki/Pleistocene" title="Pleistocene">pleistocene</a> <a href="/wiki/Megafauna" title="Megafauna">megafauna</a> along <i>ice-free corridors</i> that stretched between the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Laurentide_ice_sheet" title="Laurentide ice sheet">Laurentide</a> and <a href="/wiki/Cordilleran_Ice_Sheet" title="Cordilleran Ice Sheet">Cordilleran</a> ice sheets.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90"><a href="#cite_note-90">[90]</a></sup> Another route proposed is that, either on foot or using <a href="/wiki/Boat#History" title="Boat">primitive boats</a>, they migrated down the Pacific coast to <a href="/wiki/South_America" title="South America">South America</a> as far as <a href="/wiki/Chile" title="Chile">Chile</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-91"><a href="#cite_note-91">[91]</a></sup> Any archaeological evidence of coastal occupation during the last Ice Age would now have been covered by the <a href="/wiki/Sea_level_rise" title="Sea level rise">sea level rise</a>, up to a hundred metres since then.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-92"><a href="#cite_note-92">[92]</a></sup>  The recent finding of indigenous <a href="/wiki/Australasia" title="Australasia">Australasian</a> genetic markers in Amazonia supports the coastal route hypothesis.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-93"><a href="#cite_note-93">[93]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-94"><a href="#cite_note-94">[94]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Holocene_migrations">Holocene migrations</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Early_human_migrations&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Holocene migrations">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Meyers_map.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1625" data-file-width="2048" decoding="async" height="175" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ac/Meyers_map.jpg/220px-Meyers_map.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ac/Meyers_map.jpg/330px-Meyers_map.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ac/Meyers_map.jpg/440px-Meyers_map.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Meyers_map.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Ethnographic map of the world\'s major population groups prior to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Colonial_era" title="Colonial era">colonial era</a> ( includes the result of <a href="/wiki/Pre-modern_human_migration" title="Pre-modern human migration">pre-modern migrations</a> until the medieval period, including the <a href="/wiki/Bantu_expansion" title="Bantu expansion">Bantu</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Austronesian_people" title="Austronesian people">Austronesian</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hungarian_conquest_of_the_Carpathian_Basin" title="Hungarian conquest of the Carpathian Basin">Magyar</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Muslim_conquests" title="Muslim conquests">Arab</a>, <a href="/wiki/Early_Slavs" title="Early Slavs">Slavic</a>, <a href="/wiki/Viking_Age" title="Viking Age">Norse</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Turkic_expansion" title="Turkic expansion">Turkic</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mongol_invasions" title="Mongol invasions">Mongol</a> expansions). 4th edition of <i>Meyers Konversationslexikon</i> (1885&#8211;1892).</div></div></div>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Pre-modern_human_migration" title="Pre-modern human migration">Pre-modern human migration</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mesolithic" title="Mesolithic">Mesolithic</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Urheimat" title="Urheimat">Urheimat</a></div>\n<p>The <a href="/wiki/Holocene" title="Holocene">Holocene</a> is taken to begin 12,000 years ago, after the end of the <a href="/wiki/Last_Glacial_Maximum" title="Last Glacial Maximum">Last Glacial Maximum</a>.\nDuring the <a href="/wiki/Holocene_climatic_optimum" title="Holocene climatic optimum">Holocene climatic optimum</a>, beginning about 9,000 years ago, human populations which had been geographically confined to <a href="/wiki/Last_Glacial_Maximum_refugia" title="Last Glacial Maximum refugia">refugia</a> began to migrate.\nBy this time, most parts of the globe had been settled by <i>H. sapiens</i>; however, large areas that had been covered by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Glaciers" title="Glaciers">glaciers</a> were now re-populated.\n</p><p>This period sees the transition from the <a href="/wiki/Mesolithic" title="Mesolithic">Mesolithic</a> to the <a href="/wiki/Neolithic" title="Neolithic">Neolithic</a> stage throughout the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Temperate_zone" title="Temperate zone">temperate zone</a>.\nThe Neolithic subsequently gives way to the <a href="/wiki/Bronze_Age" title="Bronze Age">Bronze Age</a> in <a href="/wiki/Old_World" title="Old World">Old World</a> cultures and the gradual emergence of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Historical_record" title="Historical record">historical record</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Near_East" title="Ancient Near East">Near East</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bronze_Age_China" title="Bronze Age China">China</a> beginning around 4,000 years ago.\n</p><p>Large-scale migrations of the Mesolithic to Neolithic era are thought to have given rise to the pre-modern distribution of the world\'s major <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Language_families" title="Language families">language families</a> such as the <a href="/wiki/Niger%E2%80%93Congo_languages" title="Niger&#8211;Congo languages">Niger-Congo</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nilo-Saharan_languages" title="Nilo-Saharan languages">Nilo-Saharan</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Afro-Asiatic_languages" title="Afro-Asiatic languages">Afro-Asiatic</a>,  <a href="/wiki/Uralic_languages" title="Uralic languages">Uralic</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sino-Tibetan_languages" title="Sino-Tibetan languages">Sino-Tibetan</a> or <a href="/wiki/Indo-European_languages" title="Indo-European languages">Indo-European</a> phyla. The speculative <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nostratic_language" title="Nostratic language">Nostratic theory</a> postulates the derivation of the major language families of Eurasia (excluding Sino-Tibetan) from a single proto-languages spoken at the beginning of the Holocene period.\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Eurasia_2">Eurasia</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Early_human_migrations&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Eurasia">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Neolithic_Revolution" title="Neolithic Revolution">Neolithic Revolution</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Indo-European_expansion" title="Indo-European expansion">Indo-European expansion</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Proto-Uralic_homeland_hypotheses" title="Proto-Uralic homeland hypotheses">Proto-Uralic homeland hypotheses</a></div>\n<p>Evidence published in 2014 from genome analysis of ancient human remains suggests that the modern native populations of Europe largely descend from three distinct lineages:  "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Western_Hunter-Gatherers" title="Western Hunter-Gatherers">Western Hunter-Gatherers</a>", derivative of the Cro-Magnon population of Europe, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Early_European_Farmers" title="Early European Farmers">Early European Farmers</a> introduced to Europe from the Near East during the <a href="/wiki/Neolithic_Revolution" title="Neolithic Revolution">Neolithic Revolution</a> and\n<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ancient_North_Eurasians" title="Ancient North Eurasians">Ancient North Eurasians</a> which expanded to Europe in the context of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Indo-European_expansion" title="Indo-European expansion">Indo-European expansion</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ScienceMag4Sep14_95-0"><a href="#cite_note-ScienceMag4Sep14-95">[95]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Afroasiatic_Urheimat" title="Afroasiatic Urheimat">Afroasiatic Urheimat</a> has been placed in either Africa or Asia.\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Sub-Saharan_Africa">Sub-Saharan Africa</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Early_human_migrations&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Sub-Saharan Africa">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nilo-Saharan" title="Nilo-Saharan">Nilo-Saharan</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Niger-Congo" title="Niger-Congo">Niger-Congo</a></div>\n<p>The <a href="/wiki/Nilotic_peoples" title="Nilotic peoples">Nilotic peoples</a> are thought to be derived from an earlier undifferentiated <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Sudanic_languages" title="Eastern Sudanic languages">Eastern Sudanic</a> unity by the 3rd millennium BCE. The development of the Proto-Nilotes as a group may have been connected with their domestication of <a href="/wiki/Livestock" title="Livestock">livestock</a>. The Eastern Sudanic unity must have been considerably earlier still, perhaps around the 5th millennium BCE (while the proposed <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nilo-Saharan" title="Nilo-Saharan">Nilo-Saharan</a> unity would date to the <a href="/wiki/Upper_Paleolithic" title="Upper Paleolithic">Upper Paleolithic</a> about 15kya). The original locus of the early Nilotic speakers was presumably east of the Nile in what is now <a href="/wiki/South_Sudan" title="South Sudan">South Sudan</a>. The Proto-Nilotes of the 3rd millennium BCE were <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pastoralists" title="Pastoralists">pastoralists</a>, while their neighbors, the Proto-<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Central_Sudanic" title="Central Sudanic">Central Sudanic</a> peoples, were mostly agriculturalists.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-96"><a href="#cite_note-96">[96]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Niger-Congo" title="Niger-Congo">Niger-Congo</a> phylum is thought to have emerged around 6,000 years ago in West or Central Africa.\nIts expansion may have been associated with the expansion of Sahel agriculture in the African Neolithic period, following the desiccation of the Sahara in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/5.9_kiloyear_event" title="5.9 kiloyear event">c. 3900 BCE</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-97"><a href="#cite_note-97">[97]</a></sup>\nThe <a href="/wiki/Bantu_expansion" title="Bantu expansion">Bantu expansion</a> has spread the <a href="/wiki/Bantu_languages" title="Bantu languages">Bantu languages</a> to Central, Eastern and Southern Africa, partly replacing the indigenous populations of these regions. Beginning about 3,000 years ago, it reached South Africa\nabout 1,700 years ago.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-98"><a href="#cite_note-98">[98]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Pacific">Pacific</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Early_human_migrations&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Pacific">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Austronesian_peoples#Migration_and_dispersion" title="Austronesian peoples">Austronesian_peoples &#167;&#160;Migration_and_dispersion</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:272px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Chronological_dispersal_of_Austronesian_people_across_the_Pacific_(per_Bellwood_in_Chambers,_2008).png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3065" data-file-width="7268" decoding="async" height="114" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/52/Chronological_dispersal_of_Austronesian_people_across_the_Pacific_%28per_Bellwood_in_Chambers%2C_2008%29.png/270px-Chronological_dispersal_of_Austronesian_people_across_the_Pacific_%28per_Bellwood_in_Chambers%2C_2008%29.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/52/Chronological_dispersal_of_Austronesian_people_across_the_Pacific_%28per_Bellwood_in_Chambers%2C_2008%29.png/405px-Chronological_dispersal_of_Austronesian_people_across_the_Pacific_%28per_Bellwood_in_Chambers%2C_2008%29.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/52/Chronological_dispersal_of_Austronesian_people_across_the_Pacific_%28per_Bellwood_in_Chambers%2C_2008%29.png/540px-Chronological_dispersal_of_Austronesian_people_across_the_Pacific_%28per_Bellwood_in_Chambers%2C_2008%29.png 2x" width="270"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Chronological_dispersal_of_Austronesian_people_across_the_Pacific_(per_Bellwood_in_Chambers,_2008).png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Austronesians expansion map</div></div></div>\n<p>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Islands_of_the_Pacific" title="Islands of the Pacific">islands of the Pacific</a> were  populated between c. 1600 BCE and 1000 CE.\nThe <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lapita" title="Lapita">Lapita</a> people, who got their name from the archaeological site in Lapita, <a href="/wiki/New_Caledonia" title="New Caledonia">New Caledonia</a>, where their characteristic pottery was first discovered, were an Austronesian-speaking people who settled in Near Oceania (notably the Bismarck Archipelago in Papua New Guinea, and the Solomon Islands) around 1500 BCE, where some intermingling  with the existing Papuan population took place. Acquiring long distance voyaging skills, they ventured into \'Remote Oceania\', probably settling Vanuatu and New Caledonia around 1200 BCE, then Fiji, Samoa and Tonga. By the beginning of the 1st millennium BCE, this western part of Polynesia was a loose web of thriving populations settled on the islands\' coasts and living off the sea. By 0 CE <a href="/wiki/Micronesia" title="Micronesia">Micronesia</a> was completely colonized; tropical eastern Polynesia, including Tahiti, was probably settled by 700 CE. The last region of <a href="/wiki/Polynesia" title="Polynesia">Polynesia</a> to be reached was <a href="/wiki/New_Zealand" title="New Zealand">New Zealand</a>, probably by 1300 CE.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-99"><a href="#cite_note-99">[99]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Caribbean">Caribbean</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Early_human_migrations&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Caribbean">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<p>The <a href="/wiki/Caribbean" title="Caribbean">Caribbean</a> was one of the last places in the Americas that were settled by humans. The oldest remains are known from the Greater Antilles (Cuba and Hispaniola) dating between 4000&#8211;3500 BCE, and comparisons between tool-technologies suggest that these peoples moved across the Yucat&#225;n Channel from Central America. All evidence suggests that later migrants from 2000 BCE and onwards originated from South America, via the Orinoco region. The descendants of these migrants include the ancestors of the <a href="/wiki/Ta%C3%ADno" title="Ta&#237;no">Ta&#237;no</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kalinago" title="Kalinago">Kalinago</a> (Island Carib) peoples. <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-100"><a href="#cite_note-100">[100]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Arctic">Arctic</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Early_human_migrations&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Arctic">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Circumpolar_peoples" title="Circumpolar peoples">Circumpolar peoples</a></div>\n<p>The last region to be permanently settled by  human migrations is the <a href="/wiki/Arctic" title="Arctic">Arctic</a>.\n</p><p>The earliest inhabitants of North America\'s central and eastern Arctic are referred to as the <a href="/wiki/Arctic_small_tool_tradition" title="Arctic small tool tradition">Arctic small tool tradition</a> (AST) and existed c. 2500 BCE. AST consisted of several <a href="/wiki/Paleo-Eskimo" title="Paleo-Eskimo">Paleo-Eskimo</a> cultures, including the <a href="/wiki/Independence_I_culture" title="Independence I culture">Independence cultures</a> and <a href="/wiki/Pre-Dorset" title="Pre-Dorset">Pre-Dorset</a> culture.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hoffecker_101-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hoffecker-101">[101]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gibbon_102-0"><a href="#cite_note-Gibbon-102">[102]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Inuit" title="Inuit">Inuit</a> are the descendants of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Thule_culture" title="Thule culture">Thule culture</a>, which emerged from western Alaska around AD 1000 and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Inuit_expansion" title="Inuit expansion">gradually displaced</a> the Dorset culture.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-103"><a href="#cite_note-103">[103]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-104"><a href="#cite_note-104">[104]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="See_also">See also</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Early_human_migrations&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: See also">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="div-col columns column-width" style="-moz-column-width: 30em; -webkit-column-width: 30em; column-width: 30em;">\n<ul><li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Historical_migration" title="Historical summary</a></span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-extinction-10"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-extinction_10-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Homo_erectus_soloensis" title="Homo erectus soloensis">Homo erectus soloensis</a></i>, found in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Java_(island)" title="Java (island)">Java</a>, is considered the latest known specimen of <i>H. erectus</i>.\nFormerly dated to as late as 50,000 to 40,000 years ago, a 2011 study pushed back the date of its extinction of <i>H. e. soloensis</i> to 143,000 years ago at the latest, more likely before 550,000 years ago.\nIndriati E, Swisher CC III, Lepre C, Quinn RL, Suriyanto RA, et al. 2011 The Age of the 20 Meter Solo River Terrace, Java, Indonesia and the Survival of Homo erectus in Asia. <a class="external text" href="http://www.plosone.org/article/info%3Adoi%2F10.1371%2Fjournal.pone.0021562" rel="nofollow">PLoS ONE 6(6): e21562. </a> <a href="/wiki/Digital_object_identifier" title="Digital object identifier">doi</a>:<a class="external text" href="https://doi.org/10.1371%2Fjournal.pone.0021562" A weighted average age places these Middle Stone Age artefacts and fossils at 315&#8201;&#177;&#8201;34 thousand years ago. Support is obtained through the recalculated uranium series with electron spin resonance date of 286&#8201;&#177;&#8201;32 thousand years ago for a tooth from the Irhoud 3 hominin mandible.";\n<cite class="citation journal">Smith TM, Tafforeau P, Reid DJ,  et al. Estimated split times given in the source cited (in kya): Human-Neanderthal: 530&#8211;690, Deep Human [H. sapiens]: 250&#8211;360, NKSP-SKSP: 150&#8211;190, Out of Africa (OOA): 70&#8211;120.</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-25"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-25">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Rito T, Richards MB, Fernandes V, Alshamali F, Cerny V, Pereira L, Soares P., "The first modern human dispersals across Africa", <i>PLoS One</i> 2013 Nov 13; 8(11):e80031. <a href="/wiki/Digital_object_identifier" title="Digital object identifier">doi</a>:<a class="external text" href="https://doi.org/10.1371%2Fjournal.pone.0080031" rel="nofollow">10.1371/journal.pone.0080031</a>.\n"By ~130 ka two distinct groups of anatomically modern humans co-existed in Africa: broadly, the ancestors of many modern-day Khoe and San populations in the south and a second central/eastern African group that includes the ancestors of most extant worldwide populations. Early modern human dispersals correlate with climate changes, particularly the tropical African &#8220;megadroughts&#8221; of MIS 5 (marine isotope stage 5, 135&#8211;75 ka) which paradoxically may have facilitated expansions in central and eastern Africa, ultimately triggering the dispersal out of Africa of people carrying haplogroup L3 ~60 ka. Two south to east migrations are discernible within haplogroup L0. One, between 120 and 75 ka, represents the first unambiguous long-range modern human dispersal detected by mtDNA and might have allowed the dispersal of several markers of modernity. A second one, within the last 20 ka signalled by L0d, may have been responsible for the spread of southern click-consonant languages to eastern Africa, contrary to the view that these eastern examples constitute relicts of an ancient, much wider distribution."</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-26"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-26">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Jarvis JP, Scheinfeldt LB, Soi S, Lambert C, Omberg L, Ferwerda B, et al. Botigu&#233;, L.R.; Civit, S.; Arenas, C.; &#193;vila-Arcos, M.C.; Bustamante, C.D.;  et al. (2012). <a class="external text" href="//www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pmc/articles/PMC3474783" rel="nofollow">"North African Populations Carry the Signature of Admixture with Neandertals"</a>. <i>PLoS ONE</i>. <b>7</b> (10): e47765. <a href="/wiki/Bibcode" title="Bibcode">Bibcode</a>:<a class="external text" href="http://adsabs.harvard.edu/abs/2012PLoSO...747765S" rel="nofollow">2012PLoSO...747765S</a>. <a href="/wiki/Digital_object_identifier" title="Digital object identifier">doi</a>:<a class="external text" href="//doi.org/10.1371%2Fjournal.pone.0047765" rel="nofollow">10.1371/journal.pone.0047765</a>. <a href="/wiki/PubMed_Central" title="PubMed Central">PMC</a>&#160;<span class="cs1-lock-free" title="Freely accessible"><a class="external text" href="//www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pmc/articles/PMC3474783" rel="nofollow">3474783</a></span>. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/PubMed_Identifier" title="PubMed Identifier">PMID</a>&#160;<a class="external text" href="//www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pubmed/23082212" rel="nofollow">23082212</a>. <q>We found that North African populations have a significant excess of derived alleles shared with Neandertals, when compared to sub-Saharan Africans. This excess is similar to that found in non-African humans, a fact that can be interpreted as a sign of Neandertal admixture. Furthermore, the Neandertal\'s genetic signal is higher in populations with a local, pre-Neolithic North African ancestry. Therefore, the detected ancient admixture is not due to recent Near Eastern or European migrations. Sub-Saharan populations are the only ones not affected by the admixture event with Neandertals.</q></cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal&amp;rft.genre=article&amp;rft.jtitle=PLoS+ONE&amp;rft.atitle=North+African+Populations+Carry+the+Signature+of+Admixture+with+Neandertals&amp;rft.volume=7&amp;rft.issue=10&amp;rft.pages=e47765&amp;rft.date=2012&amp;rft_id=%2F%2Fwww.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov%2Fpmc%2Farticles%2FPMC3474783&amp;rft_id=info%3Apmid%2F23082212&amp;rft_id=info%3Adoi%2F10.1371%2Fjournal.pone.0047765&amp;rft_id=info%3Abibcode%2F2012PLoSO...747765S&amp;rft.aulast=S%C3%A1nchez-Quinto&amp;rft.aufirst=F.&amp;rft.au=Botigu%C3%A9%2C+L.R.&amp;rft.au=Civit%2C+S.&amp;rft.au=Arenas%2C+C.&amp;rft.au=%C3%81vila-Arcos%2C+M.C.&amp;rft.au=Bustamante%2C+C.D.&amp;rft_id=%2F%2Fwww.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov%2Fpmc%2Farticles%2FPMC3474783&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AEarly+human+migrations"></span><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r879151008" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/></span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-79"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-79">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation journal">Yotova, Vania;  et al.
